They were often recruited by kidnapping or deception to serve as sex slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fackler2007_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fackler2007-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abequestions2007_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abequestions2007-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japanparty2007_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japanparty2007-127">[127]</a></sup> Many women were raped to the point of death, or killed by torture, such as having their breasts sliced off or having their abdomens slit open.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Each slave was reportedly raped "an average of 10 rapes per day (considered by some to be a low estimate), for a five-day work week; this figure can be extrapolated to estimate that each 'comfort girl' was raped around 50 times per week or 2,500 times per year. For three years of service – the average – a comfort girl would have been raped 7,500 times." (Parker, 1995 United Nations Commissions on Human Rights)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_World_War_II">After World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_slavery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: After World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_slavery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yasuura_House.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="640" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Yasuura_House.jpg/220px-Yasuura_House.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Yasuura_House.jpg/330px-Yasuura_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Yasuura_House.jpg/440px-Yasuura_House.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yasuura_House.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Yasuura House, one such center</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Recreation_and_Amusement_Association" title="Recreation and Amusement Association">Recreation and Amusement Association</a></div>
<p>The <b>Recreation and Amusement Association</b> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">特殊慰安施設協会</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>Tokushu Ian Shisetsu Kyōkai (Special Comfort Facility Association)</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span> (<b>RAA</b>) was the largest of the organizations established by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupied_Japan" title="Occupied Japan">Japanese government</a> to provide organized <a href="/wiki/Prostitution" title="Prostitution">prostitution</a> and other leisure facilities for occupying Allied troops immediately following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>The RAA established its first brothel on 28 August: the Komachien in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%8Cmori" title="Ōmori">Ōmori</a>. By December 1945, the RAA owned 34 facilities, 16 of which were "comfort stations". The total number of prostitutes employed by the RAA amounted to 55,000 at its peak.</p>
<p>The dispersal of prostitution made it harder for GHQ to control STDs and also caused an increase in rapes by GIs, from an average of 40 a day before the SCAP order to an estimated 330 per day immediately after.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="During_the_Korean_War">During the Korean War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_slavery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: During the Korean War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prostitutes_in_South_Korea_for_the_U.S._military" title="Prostitutes in South Korea for the U.S. military">Prostitutes in South Korea for the U.S. military</a></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, the South Korean military institutionalized a "special comfort unit" similar to the one used by the Japanese military during World War II, kidnapping and pressing several North Korean women into sexual slavery. Until recently, very little was known about this apart from testimonies of retired generals and soldiers who had fought in the war. In February 2002, Korean sociologist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kim_Kwi-ok&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kim Kwi-ok (page does not exist)">Kim Kwi-ok</a> wrote the first scholarly work on Korea's comfort women through official records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>The South Korean "comfort" system was organized around three operations. First, there were "special comfort units" called T'uksu Wiandae (특수위안대, 特殊慰安隊), which operated from seven different stations. Second, there were mobile units of comfort women that visited barracks. Third, there were prostitutes who worked in private brothels that were hired by the military. Although it is still not clear how recruitment of these comfort women were organized in the South, South Korean agents were known to have kidnapped some of the women from the North.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Chunghee_Sarah_Soh" title="Chunghee Sarah Soh">Chunghee Sarah Soh</a>, the South Korean military's use of comfort women has produced "virtually no societal response," despite the country's women's movement's support for Korean comfort women within the Japanese military. Both Kim and Soh argue that this system is a legacy of Japanese colonialism, as many of Korea's army leadership were trained by the Japanese military. Both the Korean and Japanese militaries referred to these comfort women as "military supplies" in official documents and personal memoirs. The South Korean armed forces also used the same arguments as the Japanese military to justify the use of comfort women, viewing them as a "necessary social evil" that would raise soldiers' morale and prevent rape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Present_day">Present day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_slavery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Present day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Official estimates of individuals in sexual slavery worldwide vary. In 2001 the <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Migration" title="International Organization for Migration">International Organization for Migration</a> estimated 400,000, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> estimated 700,000 and <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> estimated 1.75 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_slavery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amsterdam_red_light_district.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="512" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Amsterdam_red_light_district.jpg/220px-Amsterdam_red_light_district.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Amsterdam_red_light_district.jpg/330px-Amsterdam_red_light_district.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Amsterdam_red_light_district.jpg/440px-Amsterdam_red_light_district.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amsterdam_red_light_district.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/De_Wallen" title="De Wallen">De Wallen</a> red-light district in Amsterdam. Most of the trafficked girls and women come from central and eastern Europe.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, the Bureau of the Dutch Rapporteur on Trafficking in Human Beings in 2005 estimated that there are from 1,000 to 7,000 trafficking victims a year. Most police investigations relate to legal sex businesses, with all sectors of prostitution being well represented, but with window brothels being particularly overrepresented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Dutch news site Expatica reported that in 2008, there were 809 registered trafficking victims in the Netherlands; out of those 763 were women and at least 60 percent of them were reportedly forced to work in the <a href="/wiki/Sex_industry" title="Sex industry">sex industry</a>. Of reported victims, those from <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> were all female and all forced into prostitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Germany, the trafficking of women from <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> is often organized by people from that same region. German authorities identified 676 sex-trafficking victims in 2008, compared with 689 in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The German Federal Police Office <a href="/wiki/Federal_Criminal_Police_Office_(Germany)" title="Federal Criminal Police Office (Germany)">BKA</a> reported in 2006 a total of 357 completed investigations of human trafficking, with 775 victims. Thirty-five percent of the suspects were Germans born in Germany and 8% were German citizens born outside Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bka_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-bka-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, according to NGO estimates in 2008, there may be a total 13,000–14,000 trafficking victims of all types in the country at any given time. Major countries of origin for trafficking victims brought into Greece include Nigeria, Ukraine, Russia, Bulgaria, Albania, Moldova, Romania, and Belarus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, the police estimated in 2006 that there may be between 1,500 and 3,000 victims of all types of human trafficking. The organizers and their victims generally come from Hungary, Slovakia, Romania, Ukraine, Moldova, Lithuania, Brazil, the Dominican Republic, Thailand and Cambodia, and, to a lesser extent, Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, in 2007, prosecutors handled a total of 418 trafficking cases, including 219 economic exploitation and 168 sexual exploitation cases. In the same year, the federal judicial police handled 196 trafficking files, compared with 184 in 2006. In 2007 the police arrested 342 persons for smuggling and trafficking-related crimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> A recent report by RiskMonitor foundation estimated that 70% of the prostitutes who work in Belgium are from Bulgaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Austria, Vienna has the largest number of reported trafficking cases, although trafficking is also a problem in urban centers such as Graz, Linz, Salzburg, and Innsbruck. The NGO Lateinamerikanische Frauen in Oesterreich–Interventionsstelle fuer Betroffene des Frauenhandels (LEFOE-IBF) reported assisting 108 victims of all types of human trafficking in 2006, down from 151 in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, in 2007, officials identified 1,035 sex trafficking victims and 445 labor trafficking victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_slavery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sex_trafficking_in_the_United_States" title="Sex trafficking in the United States">Sex trafficking in the United States</a></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Chronicle" title="San Francisco Chronicle">San Francisco Chronicle</a></i> reported in 2006 that in the 21st century, women, mostly from <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, Southeast Asia, Eastern Europe and the former Soviet Union, are <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking_in_the_United_States" title="Human trafficking in the United States">trafficked into the United States</a> for the purposes of sexual slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> A 2006 <a href="/wiki/ABC_News" title="ABC News">ABC News</a> story stated that, contrary to existing misconceptions, American citizens may also be coerced into sex slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeenGirls2006_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-TeenGirls2006-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001 the United States State Department estimated that 50,000 to 100,000 women and girls are trafficked each year into the United States. In 2003, the State Department report estimated that a total of 18,000 to 20,000 individuals were trafficked into the United States for either forced labor or sexual exploitation. The June 2004 report estimated the total trafficked annually at between 14,500 and 17,500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The Bush administration set up 42 Justice Department task forces and spent more than $150 million on attempts to reduce human trafficking. However, in the seven years since the law was passed, the administration has identified only 1,362 victims of human trafficking brought into the United States since 2000, nowhere near the 50,000 or more per year the government had estimated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Girl’s Education &amp; Mentoring Services (GEMS), an organization based in New York, claims that the majority of girls in the sex trade were abused as children. Poverty and a lack of education play major roles in the lives of many women in the sex industry.</p>
<p>According to a report conducted by the University of Pennsylvania, anywhere from 100,000 up to 300,000 American children at any given time may be at risk of exploitation due to factors such as drug use, homelessness, or other factors connected with increased risk for commercial sexual exploitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-penn_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-penn-152">[152]</a></sup> However, the report emphasized, "The numbers presented in these exhibits do not, therefore, reflect the actual number of cases of CSEC in the United States but, rather, what we estimate to be the number of children ‘at risk’ of commercial sexual exploitation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-penn_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-penn-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2010 Trafficking in Persons report described the United States as, "a source, transit, and destination country for men, women, and children subjected to trafficking in persons, specifically forced labor, debt bondage, and forced prostitution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Sexual slavery in the United States may occur in multiple forms and in multiple venues. Sex trafficking in the United States may be present in Asian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massage_parlors" title="Massage parlors">massage parlors</a>, Mexican <a href="/wiki/Cantina" title="Cantina">cantina</a> bars, residential brothels, or street-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pimp" title="Pimp">pimp</a>-controlled prostitution. The anti-trafficking community in the United States is debating the extent of sexual slavery. Some groups argue that exploitation is inherent in the act of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_sex" title="Commercial sex">commercial sex</a>, while other groups take a stricter approach to defining sexual slavery, considering an element of force, fraud or coercion to be necessary for sex slavery to exist.</p>
<p>The prostitutes in illegal massage parlors may be forced to work out of apartment complexes for many hours a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2010_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-May2010-154">[154]</a></sup> Many clients may not realize that some of the women who work in these massage sex parlors are actually forced in prostitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2010_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-May2010-154">[154]</a></sup> The women may initially be lured into the US under false pretenses. In huge debt to their 'owners', they are forced to earn enough to eventually "buy" their freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2010_154-2"><a href="#cite_note-May2010-154">[154]</a></sup> In some cases women who have been sex trafficked may be forced to undergo plastic surgery or abortions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> A chapter in <i>The Slave Next Door</i> (2009) reports that human trafficking and sexual enslavement are not limited to any specific location or social class. It concludes that individuals in society need to be alert to report suspicious behavior, because the psychological and physical abuse occurs which can often leave a victim unable to escape on their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2000 Congress created the Victims of Trafficking and Violence Protection Act with tougher punishments for sex traffickers. It provides for the possibility for former sex slaves to obtain a T-1 visa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2010_154-3"><a href="#cite_note-May2010-154">[154]</a></sup> To obtain the visa women must, "prove they were enslaved by 'force, fraud or coercion'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2010_154-4"><a href="#cite_note-May2010-154">[154]</a></sup> The visa allows former victims of sex trafficking to stay in the United States for 3 years and then apply for a green card.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2010_154-5"><a href="#cite_note-May2010-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Fundamentalist_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-Day_Saints" title="Fundamentalist Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-Day Saints">Fundamentalist Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-Day Saints</a> (FLDS) has been suspected of trafficking underage women across state lines, as well as across the US–Canada<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctv_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-ctv-157">[157]</a></sup> and US–Mexico borders,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> for the purpose of sometimes involuntary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plural_marriage" title="Plural marriage">plural marriage</a> and sexual abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-globe_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-globe-159">[159]</a></sup> The FLDS is suspected by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mounted_Police" title="Royal Canadian Mounted Police">Royal Canadian Mounted Police</a> of having trafficked more than 30 under-age girls from Canada to the United States between the late 1990s and 2006 to be entered into polygamous marriages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctv_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-ctv-157">[157]</a></sup> RCMP spokesman Dan Moskaluk said of the FLDS's activities: "In essence, it's human trafficking in connection with illicit sexual activity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sanangelo_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-sanangelo-160">[160]</a></sup> According to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vancouver_Sun" title="Vancouver Sun">Vancouver Sun</a>,</i> it's unclear whether or not Canada's anti-human trafficking statute can be effectively applied against the FLDS's pre-2005 activities, because the statute may not be able to be applied retroactively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vsun_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-vsun-161">[161]</a></sup> An earlier three-year-long investigation by local authorities in British Columbia into allegations of sexual abuse, human trafficking, and forced marriages by the FLDS resulted in no charges, but did result in legislative change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hb_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-hb-162">[162]</a></sup> Former FLDS members have also alleged that children belonging to the sect were forced to perform sexual acts as children upon older men while being unable to leave. This has been described by numerous former members as sexual slavery, and was reported as such by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sydney_Morning_Herald" title="Sydney Morning Herald">Sydney Morning Herald</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMH_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-164">[164]</a></sup> One former resident of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yearning_for_Zion" title="Yearning for Zion">Yearning for Zion</a>, Kathleen Mackert, stated: "I was required to perform oral sex on my father when I was seven, and it escalated from there."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMH_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_slavery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_in_modern_Africa" title="Slavery in modern Africa">Slavery in modern Africa</a></div>
<p>In Africa the <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonial</a> powers abolished slavery in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries. However, in areas outside their jurisdiction, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdist</a> empire in <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, the practice continued to thrive. Institutional slavery has been banned worldwide, but there are numerous reports of women sex slaves in areas without effective government control, such as Sudan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> northern <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Congo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">Togo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a>, a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_prostitution" title="Religious prostitution">religious prostitution</a> known as <i>trokosi</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Ritual_servitude" title="Ritual servitude">ritual servitude</a>") forcibly keeps thousands of girls and women in traditional shrines as "wives of the gods", where priests perform the sexual function in place of the gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2014, <a href="/wiki/Boko_Haram" title="Boko Haram">Boko Haram</a> kidnapped 276 female students from <a href="/wiki/Chibok" title="Chibok">Chibok</a>, Borno. More than 50 of them soon escaped, but the remainder have not been released. Instead Shekau, who has a reward of $7 million offered by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">United States Department of State</a> since June 2013 for information leading to his capture, announced his intention of selling them into <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia_2">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_slavery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3.2519_Nepalese-mother1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="294" data-file-width="200" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6d/3.2519_Nepalese-mother1.jpg" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3.2519_Nepalese-mother1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepali</a> mother who travelled to Mumbai, India, hoping to rescue her teenage daughter from an Indian <a href="/wiki/Brothel" title="Brothel">brothel</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In January 2010, the supreme court of India stated that India is "becoming a hub" for large-scale child prostitution rackets. It suggested setting up of a special investigating agency to tackle the growing problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Indiabecoming2010_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Indiabecoming2010-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>An article about the Rescue Foundation in <i>New Internationalist</i> magazine states that "according to Save the Children India, clients now prefer 10- to 12-year-old girls". The same article attributes the rising number of prostitutes believed to have contracted <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> in India’s brothels as a factor in India becoming the country with the second-largest number of people living with HIV/AIDS in the world, behind South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Women_and_Child_Development" title="Ministry of Women and Child Development">Ministry of Women and Child Development</a> estimated that there are around 2.8 million <a href="/wiki/Sex_worker" title="Sex worker">sex workers</a> in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, with 35.47 percent of them entering the trade before the age of 18 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> The number of prostitutes has also doubled in the recent decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> One news article states that an estimated 200,000 <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepalese</a> girls have been trafficked to red light areas of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Nepalese women and girls, especially virgins, are reportedly favoured in India because of their fair skin and young looks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> One report estimates that every year between 5,000 and 7,000 Nepalese girls are trafficked into the red light districts in Indian cities, and that many of the girls may only be 9 or 10 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, young girls have been sold by their families to big-city brothel owners. Often this happens due to poverty or debt, whereby the family has no other way to raise the money than to sell the young girl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bushell_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bushell-182">[182]</a></sup> Cases have also been reported where wives and sisters have been sold to brothels to raise money for gambling, drinking or drug addictions. Sex slaves are reportedly also bought by 'agents' in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> who trick young girls into coming to Pakistan for well-paying jobs. Once in Pakistan they are taken to brothels (called <i>kharabat</i>) and forced into sexual slavery, some for many years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Beardless young boys in Afghanistan may be sold as <a href="/wiki/Bacha_bazi" title="Bacha bazi">bacha bazi</a> for use in dancing and prostitution (<a href="/wiki/Pederasty" title="Pederasty">pederasty</a>), and are sometimes valued in tens of thousands of dollars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Thailand, the Health System Research Institute reported in 2005 that children in prostitution make up 40% of Thailand's prostitutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicri.it_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-unicri.it-11">[11]</a></sup> It said that a proportion of prostitutes over the age of 18, including foreign nationals mostly from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>, China's <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> province, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> and Cambodia, are also in some state of forced sexual servitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> The Tourism Police Bureau in 1997 stated that there were 500 Chinese and 200 European women in prostitution in Bangkok, many of whom entered Thailand illegally, often through Burma and Laos. Earlier reports, however, suggest different figures. (Police Colonel Sanit Meephan, deputy chief of Tourism Police Bureau, "Thailand popular haunt for foreign prostitutes," The Nation, 15 January 1997)</p>
<p>Part of the challenge in quantifying and eliminating sexual slavery in Thailand and Asia generally is the high rate of police corruption in the region. There are documented cases where Thai and other area law enforcement officials worked with human traffickers, even to the extent of returning escaped child sex slaves to brothels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-My_Name_Lon_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-My_Name_Lon-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East">Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_slavery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Trafficking in Persons Report of 2007 from the US Department of State says that sexual slavery exists in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_states_of_the_Persian_Gulf" title="Arab states of the Persian Gulf">Arab states of the Persian Gulf</a>, where women and children may be trafficked from the <a href="/wiki/Post-Soviet_states" title="Post-Soviet states">post-Soviet states</a>, Eastern Europe, <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a>, Africa, <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> or other parts of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to media reports from late 2014 the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a> (ISIL) was <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_21st-century_Islamism#By_ISIS" title="Slavery in 21st-century Islamism">selling Yazidis and Christian women as slaves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> According to Haleh Esfandiari of the <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson_International_Center_for_Scholars" title="Woodrow Wilson International Center for Scholars">Woodrow Wilson International Center for Scholars</a>, after ISIL militants have captured an area "[t]hey usually take the older women to a makeshift slave market and try to sell them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> In mid-October 2014 the U.N. estimated that 5,000 to 7,000 Yazidi women and children were abducted by ISIL and sold into slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the digital magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Dabiq_(magazine)" title="Dabiq (magazine)">Dabiq</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">ISIL</a> claimed religious justification for enslaving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yazidi" title="Yazidi">Yazidi</a> women whom they consider to be from a heretical sect. ISIL claimed that the Yazidi are idol worshipers and their enslavement part of the old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shariah" title="Shariah">shariah</a> practice of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spoils_of_war" title="Spoils of war">spoils of war</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> ISIL appealed to <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">apocalyptic beliefs</a> and "claimed justification by a Hadith that they interpret as portraying the revival of slavery as a precursor to the end of the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> In late September 2014, 126 Islamic scholars from around the Muslim world signed an <a href="/wiki/Open_letter" title="Open letter">open letter</a> to the Islamic State's leader <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_al-Baghdadi" title="Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi">Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi</a>, rejecting his group's interpretations of the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Qur'an</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> to justify its actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christianpost-2014-09-25_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-christianpost-2014-09-25-204">[204]</a></sup> The letter accuses the group of instigating <a href="/wiki/Fitna_(word)" title="Fitna (word)">fitna</a>—sedition—by instituting slavery under its rule in contravention of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_slavery#Modern_interpretations" title="Islamic views on slavery">anti-slavery consensus</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">Islamic scholarly community</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OpenLetToAlBagh_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-OpenLetToAlBagh-205">[205]</a></sup> In late 2014 ISIL released a pamphlet on the treatment of female slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> In January 2015, further rules for sex slaves were announced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>Selling women and children still occurs in the Middle East. "IS [Islamic State] offers women and underage children in a kind of virtual slave market with for-sale photos. ... The transfer of money, as the reporter discovered, takes place through a liaison office in Turkey".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> Yazidi women have also reported being raped and used as sexual slave by members of ISIS. In November 2015 it was reported that "around 2,000 women and girls are still being bought and sold in ISIS-controlled areas. The young become sex slaves and older women are beaten and used as house slaves, according to survivors and accounts from ISIS militants".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_slavery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.unescobkk.org/fileadmin/user_upload/culture/Trafficking/project/Graph_Worldwide_Sept_2004.pdf" rel="nofollow">UNESCO Trafficking Project</a>. unescobkk.org.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Rome-Statute-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Rome-Statute_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Rome-Statute_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation">"Articles 7 and 8", <a class="external text" href="http://legal.un.org/icc/statute/99_corr/cstatute.htm" rel="nofollow"><i>Rome Statute</i></a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+slavery&amp;rft.atitle=Articles+7+and+8&amp;rft.btitle=Rome+Statute&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Flegal.un.org%2Ficc%2Fstatute%2F99_corr%2Fcstatute.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">However the elements of the crime of sexual enslavement are described in more detail in a separate document originating from Article 9 of the Rome Statute: "General introduction 1. Pursuant to article 9 [of the Rome Statute], the following Elements of Crimes shall assist the Court in the interpretation and application of articles 6, 7 and 8, consistent with the Statute" (Article 1 of the Elements of the Crime). They are found in a paragraphs entitled "Article 7 (1) (g)-2 Crime against humanity of sexual slavery"; "Article 8 (2) (b) (xxii)-2 War crime of sexual slavery"; and "Article 8 (2) (e) (vi)-2 War crime of sexual slavery". The same wording is used in all three paragraphs (<cite class="citation">"Article 7 (1) (g)-2 Crime against humanity of sexual slavery", <a class="external text" href="http://www.iclklamberg.com/Elements.htm#Article%207%281%29%28g%29-2" rel="nofollow"><i>Elements of Crime</i></a>, International Criminal Law Database &amp; Commentary<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved December 2012</span></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+slavery&amp;rft.atitle=Article+7+%281%29+%28g%29-2+Crime+against+humanity+of+sexual+slavery&amp;rft.btitle=Elements+of+Crime&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.iclklamberg.com%2FElements.htm%23Article%25207%25281%2529%2528g%2529-2&amp;rft.pub=International+Criminal+Law+Database+%26+Commentary&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> <span class="error citation-comment" style="font-size:100%">Check date values in: <code style="color:inherit; border:inherit; padding:inherit;">|access-date=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#bad_date" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span>)</span>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<dl>
<dt>Elements</dt>
</dl>
<ol>
<li>The perpetrator exercised any or all of the powers attaching to the right of ownership over one or more persons, such as by purchasing, selling, lending or bartering such a person or persons, or by imposing on them a similar deprivation of liberty.</li>
<li>The perpetrator caused such person or persons to engage in one or more acts of a sexual nature.</li>
<li>The conduct took place in the context of and was associated with an international armed conflict.</li>
<li>The perpetrator was aware of factual circumstances that established the existence of an armed conflict.</li>
</ol>
</blockquote>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Commentaries on treaties explain why certain words and phrases appeared in a treaty and what the delegates considered when agreeing to the words and phrases used.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-clift-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-clift_9-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-clift_9-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Stephen Clift; Simon Carter (2000). <i>Tourism and Sex</i>. Cengage Learning EMEA. pp. 75–78. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-85567-636-2" title="Special:BookSources/1-85567-636-2">1-85567-636-2</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+slavery&amp;rft.au=Simon+Carter&amp;rft.au=Stephen+Clift&amp;rft.btitle=Tourism+and+Sex&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1-85567-636-2&amp;rft.pages=75-78&amp;rft.pub=Cengage+Learning+EMEA&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-sheldon20-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-sheldon20_19-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-sheldon20_19-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Kerry Sheldon; Dennis Howitt (2007). <i>Sex Offenders and the Internet</i>. John Wiley and Sons. p. 20. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-470-02800-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-470-02800-9">0-470-02800-9</a>. <q>'Child pornography is not pornography in any real sense; simply the evidence recorded on film or video tape – of serious sexual assaults on young children' (Tate, 1992, p.203) ... 'Every piece of child pornography, therefore, is a record of the sexual use/abuse of the children involved.' Kelly and Scott (1993, p. 116) ... '...the record of the systematic rape, abuse, and torture of children on film and photograph, and other electronic means.' Edwards(2000, p.1)</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+slavery&amp;rft.au=Dennis+Howitt&amp;rft.au=Kerry+Sheldon&amp;rft.btitle=Sex+Offenders+and+the+Internet&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-470-02800-9&amp;rft.pages=20&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+and+Sons&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Klain-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Klain_20-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Klain_20-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Eva J. Klain; Heather J. Davies, Molly A. Hicks, ABA Center on Children and the Law (2001). <i>Child Pornography: The Criminal-justice-system Response</i>. National Center for Missing &amp; Exploited Children. <q>Because the children depicted in child pornography are often shown while engaged in sexual activity with adults or other children,they are first and foremost victims of child sexual abuse.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+slavery&amp;rft.au=Eva+J.+Klain&amp;rft.au=Heather+J.+Davies%2C+Molly+A.+Hicks%2C+ABA+Center+on+Children+and+the+Law&amp;rft.btitle=Child+Pornography%3A+The+Criminal-justice-system+Response&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pub=National+Center+for+Missing+%26+Exploited+Children&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> <span class="citation-comment" style="display:none; color:#33aa33">CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Multiple_names:_authors_list" title="Category:CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list">link</a>)</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Wortley17-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Wortley17_21-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wortley17_21-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wortley17_21-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Richard Wortley; Stephen Smallbone. "Child Pornography on the Internet". <i>Problem-Oriented Guides for Police</i>. No. 41: 17. <q>The children portrayed in child pornography are first victimized when their abuse is perpetrated and recorded. They are further victimized each time that record is accessed.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+slavery&amp;rft.atitle=Child+Pornography+on+the+Internet&amp;rft.au=Richard+Wortley&amp;rft.au=Stephen+Smallbone&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Problem-Oriented+Guides+for+Police&amp;rft.pages=17&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=No.+41"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-sheldon9-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-sheldon9_22-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-sheldon9_22-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Kerry Sheldon; Dennis Howitt (2007). <i>Sex Offenders and the Internet</i>. John Wiley and Sons. p. 9. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-470-02800-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-470-02800-9">0-470-02800-9</a>. <q>...supplying the material to meet this demand results in the further abuse of children Pictures, films and videos function as a permanent record of the original sexual abuse. Consequently, memories of the trauma and abuse are maintained as long as the record exists. Victims filmed and photographed many years ago will nevertheless be aware throughout their lifetimes that their childhood victimization continues to be exploited perversely.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+slavery&amp;rft.au=Dennis+Howitt&amp;rft.au=Kerry+Sheldon&amp;rft.btitle=Sex+Offenders+and+the+Internet&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-470-02800-9&amp;rft.pages=9&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+and+Sons&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
